Towards efficient index construction and approximate nearest neighbor search in high-dimensional spaces. Proceedings of the VLDB Endowment 16, 8 (2023), 1979–1991. Appendix A Appendix A.1. Partitioning Methods In this section, we study several different methods for generating overlapping partitions of the point-sets including: (1) random binary partitioning; (2) the RBC method described in the main text; (3) hierarchical k-means; and (4) sorting-LSH. We then experimentally evaluate our choices for partitioning across different datasets and show that RBC is a high-quality and scalable default partitioning method for PiPNN. A.1.1. Binary Partitioning Given a subproblem 𝒫 ⊆ 𝒳 \mathcal{P}\subseteq\mathcal{X} , binary partitioning selects two distinct leader points p 1 , p 2 ∈ 𝒫 p_{1},p_{2}\in\mathcal{P} at random, then dividing 𝒫 \mathcal{P} into two parts 𝒫 1 = { p ∈ 𝒫 : ∥ p , p 1 ∥ ≤ ∥ p , p 2 ∥ } \mathcal{P}{1}={p\in\mathcal{P}:\lVert p,p{1}\rVert\leq\lVert p,p_{2}\rVert} and 𝒫 2 = { p ∈ 𝒫 : ∥ p , p 1 ∥ > ∥ p , p 2 ∥ } \mathcal{P}{2}={p\in\mathcal{P}:\lVert p,p{1}\rVert>\lVert p,p_{2}\rVert} (Munoz et al., 2019) . In plain words, we assign each point to its closest leader. The two resulting sets define the points in the subsequent subproblems, which are divided recursively by applying binary partitioning. This repeats until the number of points in every subproblem is at most a predefined cluster size C C . The resulting clusters, ℬ 1 , … , ℬ b \mathcal{B}{1},\dots,\mathcal{B}{b} , we refer to as leaves. Binary partitioning is the only technique used in the HCNNG algorithm; after dividing all points into leaf clusters, HCNNG proceeds by building subgraphs on every leaf, then taking the union of these subgraphs. Because the leaves resulting from a single partitioning procedure are disjoint, the subgraphs built on them will naturally be disconnected from each other. Thus, the algorithm needs some way to connect leaf clusters together. HCNNG resolves this through the replication process described earlier in this paper. That is, it runs the partitioning and leaf-building phases many times, using different seeds for randomization in order to produce different leaf clusters, then takes the union of all resulting graphs. This connects the graph because, across many replications, an individual point will share leaves with many different sets points. While replication can be effective for combining leaves into a unified graph, it is expensive. HCNNG often requires upwards of 30 replications to produce graphs of acceptable quality. Notably, binary partitioning does not accommodate the fanout strategy in lieu of replication. A.1.2. Hierarchical k k -Means In the well-studied problem of k k -means clustering, the problem is to select a subset of k k centers from ℝ d \mathbb{R}^{d} that minimizes the sum of squared distances between any point and its nearest center. Although k k -means clustering is often difficult in high-dimensional spaces, there are many heuristic solutions that work well in practice. We implement a partitioning technique based on hierarchical k k -means by applying Alg. 5, but rather than selecting random leaders in each subproblem, we instead run a k k -Means algorithm to select k k cost-minimizing leaders which we then use for ball-carving. The remainder of the algorithm is the same, including the fanout procedure. We find that choosing leaders through k k -means yields a graph of similar (but slightly worse) quality to choosing leaders at random (see Figure 7 in Section 5). A.1.3. Sorting LSH Locality-Sensitive Hashing (LSH) is a celebrated technique for clustering points for the purpose of nearest neighbor search (Andoni et al., 2015; Pham and Liu, 2022; Charikar, 2002) . A LSH family ℋ \mathcal{H} is a family of hash functions of the form h : 𝒳 → { 0 , 1 } h:\mathcal{X}\rightarrow{0,1} , such that similar points are more likely to collide; namely, the probability P  r h ∼ ℋ  [ h  ( x ) = h  ( y ) ] Pr_{h\sim\mathcal{H}}[h(x)=h(y)] should be large when x , y ∈ 𝒳 x,y\in\mathcal{X} are similar, and small when x , y x,y are farther apart. Following (Gottesbüren et al., 2025) , we create a Sorting LSH index by hashing each point x ∈ R x\in R multiple times via independent hash functions h 1 , … , h t h_{1},...,h_{t} from ℋ \mathcal{H} , concatenating the hashes into a string ( h 1  ( x ) , h 2  ( x ) , … , h t  ( x ) ) (h_{1}(x),h_{2}(x),...,h_{t}(x)) , and then sorting the points in R R lexicographically based on these strings of hashes. We then partition into leaves by dividing the sorted sequence into consecutive groups of at most m  x mx points. Since there is no natural analog of fanout for a sorting LSH index, we rely on replication instead of fanout to place a point in multiple leaves. Although constructing a sorting LSH index is quite fast (it requires applying a number of hyper-plane tests per-point, and some parallel sorting), the extra cost of performing replication when compared to multi-level fanout offsets much of the faster computation of LSH-regions compared to dense-ball carving, to the point of actually being slower. We further find that LSH-based partitioning yields a significantly lower quality graph than k k -ball carving, either with random leaders or k k -means leaders (Figure 7). Comparison of partitioning strategies. We have now described four partitioning methods for use in PiPNN: binary partitioning, dense-ball carving with either random leaders or k k -means centroids, and Sorting-LSH. To demonstrate the merits of the partitioning methods in isolation, we fix the leaf strategy to the best performing method (bidirected k k -NN). The relative performance between partitioning strategies was similar when using other leaf methods. Fig. 7 shows the QPS-recall tradeoff of the different partitioning methods on the ablation datasets. Randomized ball carving consistently produces the best query performance. Binary partitioning and k k -means are sometimes competitive (OpenAI, BigANN). Table 2 shows the time taken by the different partitioning methods. Randomized ball carving is consistently much faster than k k -means and Sorting-LSH. Compared to binary partitioning it is substantially faster on OpenAI and Wiki, while exhibiting similar running time on BigANN and SpaceV. We omit the results for k k -means clustering on Wikipedia Cohere because it failed to complete within 24 hours. Although k k -means could be accelerated, e.g., with subsampling strategies, as the index quality is not better than ball carving we did not optimize the indexing time further. In summary, randomized ball carving consistently achieves the highest quality indexes while having the fastest construction time. Figure 7. Four different partitioning methods applied in the PiPNN algorithm to different datasets. All four methods use a 2NN-graph in the leaf-building phase. k k -means is omitted from the WikiCohere plot due to failure to finish within 24h. Table 2. Time spent on each partitioning method. Listed times are in seconds and only include the partitioning phase. A.1.4. Fanout vs. Replicas In Sec. 4.1, we explain that PiPNN's RBC method uses a single recursive process to select multiple leaders per level, referred to as multi-level fanout, instead of repeatedly replicating the entire partitioning. We showed that this significantly speeds up partitioning times Fig. 3 and had no impact on quality. We show the lack of quality impact in Fig. 8. As can be seen, the difference in QPS at 0.9 0.9 recall is marginal and without any consistent winner. Any differences in the results can likely be explained by OS jitter. A.2. Fanout vs. Replication In Section 4.1, we presented fanout and multi-level fanout as replacements to replication for strategies to create overlapping partitions. Here, we give a more thorough explanation for why fanout and multi-level fanout yield significantly better build times. Suppose we select ℓ \ell leaders in each subproblem during partitioning. Then within a given level of the recursion tree, we must obtain the distances between each point and the leaders within the subproblems in which it is contained. In other words, we compute a total of f i ⋅ | 𝒫 | ⋅ ℓ f_{i}\cdot\lvert\mathcal{P}\rvert\cdot\ell distances in the i i th level, where f i f_{i} is the aggregate fanout (i.e. how many subproblems each point is contained in) at level i i . Under the replication strategy, f i = 1 f_{i}=1 at every level, but we repeat the entire procedure some r r many times, yielding the same cost as if each point had aggregate fanout r r at every level. If we were to get an analogous partitioning under the fanout strategy, we would execute the top level only once, wherein we would assign each point to the subproblems of its r r nearest leaders. Levels beyond the first would have f i = r f_{i}=r and thus have the same number of distance computations as with replication, but the first level wpuld only call for | 𝒫 | ⋅ ℓ \lvert\mathcal{P}\rvert\cdot\ell . Multi-level fanout extends the cost reduction beyond the first level. For example, if we fanout r 1 r_{1} on the top level and r 2 r_{2} on the second level, then the second level now maintains a low aggregate fanout of r 1 r_{1} . In summary, the strategies require the following number of distance computations: r r replications r ⋅ | 𝒫 | ⋅ ℓ r\cdot\lvert\mathcal{P}\rvert\cdot\ell each level r r fanout | 𝒫 | ⋅ ℓ \lvert\mathcal{P}\rvert\cdot\ell first level, r ⋅ | 𝒫 | ⋅ ℓ r\cdot\lvert\mathcal{P}\rvert\cdot\ell thereafter r 1 , r 2 r_{1},r_{2} multi-level fanout (where r 1 ⋅ r 2 = r r_{1}\cdot r_{2}=r ) | 𝒫 | ⋅ ℓ \lvert\mathcal{P}\rvert\cdot\ell first level, r 1 ⋅ | 𝒫 | ⋅ ℓ r_{1}\cdot\lvert\mathcal{P}\rvert\cdot\ell second level, r ⋅ | 𝒫 | ⋅ ℓ r\cdot\lvert\mathcal{P}\rvert\cdot\ell thereafter As a reminder, the recursion depth is usually very low because of the high number of leaders selected in each subproblem. Thus, reducing the cost of the top 2 levels of recursion has a very large impact on overall partitioning time. This also limits how many levels we can distribute fanout across, as any fanout beyond the second level has a high likelihood to not take effect due to many subproblems already reaching their base cases. Figure 8. Given equal point repeat budget, all three methods exhibit similar QPS at fixed recall. A.3. Picking within Leaves Figure 9. Given equal point repeat budget, Multi-level Fanout greatly reduces time to partition into leaves (termed 'Buckets' here). As mentioned, PiPNN selects for each element of a leaf a subset of its co-inhabitants to prune via HashPrune. In our final algorithm we used bi-directed k-NN's for this purpose. In this section we describe all the other methods we considered, and give an ablation to demonstrate why k-NN was suitable for inclusion as the sole method used in PiPNN. A.3.1. Degree-Restricted Minimum Spanning Trees We first describe an existing leaf method developed for use in the HCNNG algorithm that we incorporate into PiPNN. The method is to build an undirected degree-restricted minimum spanning tree over the points within a leaf, where the degree of each node is capped to be at most 3 3 . We will refer to this approach simply as MST. This approach enforces connectivity on the subgraph while (1) prioritizing the shortest edges and (2) ensuring that the resulting graph is sparse. Intuitively, these short edges are important for the final stages of a greedy search, as they increase the likelihood of finding the true nearest neighbor once the search has navigated to the correct region of the graph. However, building an MST using all pairwise distances within a leaf is a performance bottleneck due to the cost of storing and sorting all pairwise edges (required e.g., when running a degree-restricted variant of Kruskal's algorithm). To solve this, the implementation of HCNNG in ParlayANN builds the MST from a sparser graph where each point is only connected to its l l -nearest neighbors (e.g., l = 10 l=10 ) within the leaf. This restriction was shown not to affect the quality of the resulting index, and yields significant performance improvements. A.3.2. k k -Nearest Neighbor Graphs Extrapolating from the idea that short-range edges are important for traversing small local neighborhoods, a very natural subgraph to build on a leaf is a k k -nearest neighbor ( k k -NN) graph. That is, for each point v v , we identify the k k nearest points within the cluster and add edges to and/or from each of those points. The resulting k k -NN graph is closely related to an MST but with a few distinctions. Most notably, in contrast to the MST, the k k -NN graph on a leaf is not necessarily connected. However, it's not clear that this property is necessary, given that leaves themselves are not connected to each other until replication/fanout adds overlapping subgraphs. In exchange, the k k -NN graph is faster to compute and more tunable. k-NN Leaf Design Space. We consider several choices for directing the edges in the k k -NN graph. In the standard notion of a k k -NN graph, which we will refer to as the Directed k-NN, we add an edge from each point to its k k nearest neighbors. Alternatively, we can flip the directions of all edges in the Inverted k-NN, in which we add an edge to each point from its k k nearest neighbors. Although unconventional, this inverted k k -NN construction may in fact be better suited to building a navigable graph than the standard k k -NN graph. This is because if a greedy traversal for a point in the dataset lands on its nearest neighbor, the only node to which it can move that makes progress to the target point is that point itself. More generally, we can describe this relationship as the following: if a traversal aims to reach a node, the nodes that the traversal is most likely to try to reach it from is its nearest neighbors. Finally, we consider the Bi-directed k-NN, the union of the directed and inverted k k -NNs. In this graph, for each point, we add edges to and from its k k nearest neighbors. We will also refer to k-NN as shorthand for this method, as we find it to be the best of the three. Our experiments ( Fig. 10) show that the inverted k k -NN strategy yields graphs of much higher quality than the directed k k -NN, but that the bi-directed k k -NN is better than both. This result suggests that the forward edges and backward edges each contribute to the traversability of the graph. Moreover, we find that building bi-directed k k -NN on leaves with the right choice of k k yields better search performance than building MSTs. Through ablations, we reveal a clear relationship between the parameter k k and search performance. Increasing k k , as one might expect, increases the average degree of each node in the graph. This makes visiting a node more costly. In exchange, it decreases the average number of nodes visited per query. As we show in Fig. 11, this results in a sweet spot for k k ranging from 2 to 4 which produces the best graphs. A.3.3. All-to-All Prune As described in Section 2.1, the RobustPrune procedure takes a set 𝒩 \mathcal{N} of candidate neighbors for a point x ∈ 𝒳 x\in\mathcal{X} and returns a subset of 𝒩 \mathcal{N} under some given size limit (Jayaram Subramanya et al., 2019) . In particular, RobustPrune attempts to choose a set of neighbors for x x from the set of candidates that ensures good navigability. For the candidate neighbors of a point x x , Vamana uses all points visited during a beam search for x x . Typically, the vast majority of points visited during beam search are in the close vicinity of the target point. Thus, these points can be argued to roughly approximate a subset of the local neighborhood around x x . Since the leaves into which a given x x falls in PiPNN also approximate the local neighborhood around x x , we consider using RobustPrune to build leaves. More specifically, for a leaf ℬ i \mathcal{B}{i} , we run RobustPrune on each x ∈ ℬ i x\in\mathcal{B}{i} , supplying ℬ i \mathcal{B}{i} as the candidate set. This yields up to m m neighbors per point in the leaf.Optimizing RobustPrune. We note that RobustPrune can be costly to run on large lists of candidates, because each time a neighbor is chosen, it considers all other candidates for removal. In PiPNN, we provide RobustPrune with several times more candidates than Vamana often does (our cluster size C C is roughly 1000). To remedy this, we use a lazy implementation of RobustPrune. This version does not remove any redundant candidates upon adding a neighbor. Instead, prior to adding a candidate c c to the neighbor list, we check all previously-admitted neighbors to see if any should have pruned away c c , then delete c c if so. Compared to the standard version, our version can potentially omit unnecessary comparisons. Specifically, of the comparisons that standard RobustPrune does, it only performs those on candidates closer to the target than the last neighbor added to the output neighbor list.Comparison of Prune Kernels. We evaluate five different methods for pruning the candidate lists induced by the partitioning process: building a bi-directed minimum spanning tree, RobustPrune, building a k k -NN graph, building an inverted k k -NN graph, and a bi-directed k k -NN graph. Figure 10 shows the performance of these different leaf methods, keeping the rest of the pipeline fixed, on the ablation datasets.Figure 10. Comparing QPS-recall trade-offs of leaf pruning methods. Bi-directed k k -NN ( k k -NN) performs best. Table 3. Average degree for each method for each ablation dataset. Degree is capped at 64 for all methods.We observe that bi-directing the k k -NN graphs is critical for obtaining good performance, but between bidirected k k -NN and bidirected MST, performance is similar; bidirected k k -NN outperforms bidirected MST in some high recall situations due to the tunability of its density. RobustPrune has the disadvantage of producing much denser graphs, as can be seen in Table 3. This ends up producing indexes that require fewer visited nodes at high recall, but the increased number of comparisons when visiting a node outweighs the benefit. RobustPrune in the leaves produces candidate lists that are unlikely to be further sparsified by either HashPrune or the final application of RobustPrune. Thus RobustPrune produces graphs with average degree close to the maximum degree (55.45 on average).Tuning k k -NN. By increasing the value of the parameter k k , we can raise the density of the subgraph produced in each leaf. This increases the cost of visiting a node while decreasing the number of steps taken during a query. Fig. 11 displays this effect on MS-SPACEV across k ∈ [ 1 , 10 ] k\in[1,10] . The behavior is similar on other datasets. When increasing k k , the average number of visited nodes per search goes down sharply at first but with quickly diminishing returns. The initial drop is indicative of the value of these first few short edges to the general navigability of the graph – that is, only having an edge to one's nearest neighbor is insufficient. However, after only a few increments of k k , the additional edges no longer clearly improve the speed at which queries converge to the target. On the other hand, the additional edges do continue to steadily increase the number of distance computations due to further densifying the graph.In summary, the effects of increasing k k from 1 1 to 10 10 have a significant impact on overall query performance. Increasing k k from 1 1 to { 2 , 3 } {2,3} yields an immediate increase to query throughput, but subsequent increases yield a steady decline in throughput due to adding redundant edges. Based on these results, we set k = 2 k=2 as a sane default, although 3 or 4 would also suffice.Figure 11. Varying the parameter k k for k k -NN graphs in leaves.A.4. Ablation of Leaf OptimizationsFigure 12. Effect of leaf optimizations on total leaf building times. (D) denotes pre-computing the distance matrix. (E) denotes pre-computing the distance matrix with the Eigen library. (VQ) denotes using VQSort or VQPartialSort to sort distances. Data is normalized with respect to the fastest build time; lower is better.In Section 5.1, we described several optimizations for building leaves in PiPNN, namely building distance matrices, computing those matrices using the Eigen library (Guennebaud et al., 2010) , and finding near neighbors using the Highway library's vectorized partial sort (Google, 2026) .We show the effect of these optimizations in Figure 12 on the ablation datasets. We compare naive implementations of k k -NN as well as All-to-All Prune (wherein we perform RobustPrune within each leaf to generate candidates for HashPrune) against methods optimized by first building a distance matrix (D), building a distance matrix with the Eigen library (D,E), and methods which additionally use VQSort or VQPartialSort for identifying near-neighbors. Building a distance matrix gives average speedups of 2.44 × 2.44\times and 4.95 × 4.95\times for k k -NN and All-to-All Prune respectively. Doing so with Eigen gives a further 8.57 × 8.57\times and 1.98 × 1.98\times speedup for the two methods. All-to-All Prune benefits marginally from using VQSort ( 1.05 × 1.05\times speedup), whereas k k -NN does not get further improvement compared to the priority queue approach. All optimizations combined give our final versions of k k -NN and All-to-All Prune 27.03 × 27.03\times and 12.71 × 12.71\times speedups respectively on average.A.5. Intermediate and Final PruningFigure 13. Parameter sweep over hash-family sizes in the range of [ 6 , 16 ] [6,16] on SPACEV (100M) dataset, without any final prune. All values between 8 and 16 are suitable.HashPrune. We developed the HashPrune procedure to ensure that memory usage was bounded regardless of the amount of replication or the degree in a leaf. On the one hand, this is necessary to build high quality indexes on billion-scale datasets where memory requirements are high, but HashPrune also ensures that memory can be allocated up-front in a single contiguous block. Thus, the procedure not only enables better scalability, but it also avoids dynamically resizing the intermediate candidate lists.To test whether limiting the candidates during construction impacts query performance, we compared reservoir sizes of 64, 128, and 192 against an unbounded degree intermediate graph on the ablation datasets (with final prune performed down to 64 max degree). We observed that HashPrune has almost no impact on the distance computations performed (and, thus, the QPS achieved) to achieve high recall when paired with the bi-directed k k -NN method we use for candidate picking candidates within buckets. These results are displayed for MS-SPACEV-100M in Tab. 5. This table also shows the marginal variation across different m m parameters for the hash functions. This surprising parameter insensitivity is likely caused by our use of a final prune.Final Prune. PiPNN employs a final RobustPrune on each candidate list after the partitions have been processed. We found this to improve QPS by an average of 10.18 % 10.18% and 9.45 % 9.45% at 0.9 and 0.95 recall respectively across the four ablation datasets. We find that the final pruning step comprises an average of 9.82 % 9.82% of the total build time across the four ablation datasets. This number drops to just 6.86 % 6.86% for the 100M sized ablation datasets, where the other indexing steps are more costly. Due to its quality improvements, and given its relatively modest impact to the overall construction time, we find that final pruning is beneficial and always enable it.A.6. PiPNN vs. Recent ANNS MethodsWe were unable to evaluate MIRAGE (Voruganti and Özsu, 2025) and FastKCNA (Yang et al., 2024) on billion scale datasets due to excessive memory usage. We had to modify MIRAGE to run on 100M datasets, as described in Sec. A.9, but did not need to require FastKCNA. We show results for our two 100M sized datasets used in our PiPNN ablation in Fig. 15. FastKCNA achieves slightly worse query performance than PiPNN but with builds times at least 17.3 × 17.3\times longer. MIRAGE was unable to achieve competitive performance, and had build times at least 19.1 × 19.1\times longer then PiPNN.Fig. 14 shows results for the methods able to run the billion-scale Text2Image dataset (Simhadri et al., 2022) comprised of 200-dimensional float vectors outfitted with the MIPS dissimilarity measure. HNSW is not displayed due to being unable to achieve 0.8 recall. PiPNN performs better than other methods with respect to maximum achieved recall.Figure 14. PiPNN achieves the highest recall on the difficult Text2Image dataset.Figure 15. Results for the 100M sized ablation datasets MS-SPACEV-100M and BigANN-100M comparing PiPNN against new recent methods for scaleable index build. Table 4. Average hardware counters from perf per method and dataset. Values are shown in hundred billions ( × 10 11 ) (\times 10^{11}) for cycles, instructions, and LLC-load-misses. Pre-fetching is disabled for Vamana in this experiment. PiPNN indexes datasets in fewer cycles and with fewer cache-misses.Table 5. Average cmps for Recall ¿ 0.95 (SPACEV-100M Dataset)A.7. Asymptotic Analysis of PiPNNWe now consider the expected running time to build a PiPNN index. First, we point out that although in some adversarial input sets certain leader choices may lead to locally-unbalanced partitioning across subproblems, it is reasonable in practice to assume that randomized ball carving divides points across subproblems roughly evenly in expectation. Under this assumption, recursive ball carving with ℓ \ell random leaders in each subproblem terminates within O  ( log ℓ  n ) O(\log{\ell}n) levels with high probability, where n n is the number of input points. Within each level, up to f  n fn instances of points each undergo d d -dimensional distance computations with the ℓ \ell leaders in its subproblem (where f f is the amount of fanout performed at the prior level of recursion). It follows that partitioning takes O  ( d  f  n  ℓ  log ℓ  ( n ) ) O(dfn\ell\log_{\ell}(n)) time with high probability. We stop partitioning when every leaf contains at most c c points. We subsequently spend O  ( | ℬ i | 2  d ) O(|\mathcal{B}{i}|^{2}d) time for each leaf ℬ i \mathcal{B}{i} building a | ℬ i | × | ℬ i | |\mathcal{B}{i}|\times|\mathcal{B}{i}| distance matrix and adding edges between each point in ℬ i \mathcal{B}{i} and the two points from which it has smallest distance. Thus, we can charge each point in ℬ i \mathcal{B}{i} for up to O  ( | ℬ i |  d ) ≤ O  ( c  d ) O(|\mathcal{B}{i}|d)\leq O(cd) time. In total, there are ≤ f  n \leq fn instances of points across all leaves, so leaf-building in total takes O  ( c  d  f  n ) O(cdfn) time.HashPrune maintains a reservoir of up to O  ( m ) O(m) candidate neighbors for each point, where m m is the target maximum degree of the final graph. Recall from the leaf-building step that each of the ≤ f  n \leq fn instances of points adds a candidate edge to and from each of its two nearest in-leaf neighbors. Thus, at most 4  f  n 4fn candidate neighbors are passed into HashPrune across all points. We hash each one via LSH against roughly 12 random vectors using 12 O  ( d ) O(d) -time distance computations. We subsequently compare its distance from the target point with that of the furthest candidate in the reservoir (via brute force in O  ( m ) O(m) time) and the candidate already in its hash bucket if one exists (via binary search in O  ( log  m ) O(\log m) time). Thus, HashPrune consumes O  ( f  n  m ) O(fnm) time.Finally, having used HashPrune to limit the number of candidates to O  ( m ) O(m) per point, RobustPrune takes only O  ( m 2 ) O(m^{2}) time for each point. Thus, the final prune phase takes O  ( n  m 2 ) O(nm^{2}) time.Combining all components of the algorithm produces a total expected time complexity of O  ( n  ( d  f  ℓ  log ℓ  ( n ) + c  d  f + f  m + m 2 ) ) O(n(df\ell\log{\ell}(n)+cdf+fm+m^{2})) . A.8. Proof of Determinism of PiPNN The determinism of PiPNN follows from two propositions. Firstly, the partitioning produced by Algorithm 5 is deterministic. Thus the candidates which are given to HashPrune are always the same (though the order that they are merged into candidate lists by way of HashPrune is non-deterministic). Secondly, HashPrune itself is history independent, and so the order that points has no bearing on the final adjacency lists for each point. Thus PiPNN is deterministic. We prove the each proposition in this section. Lemma A.1. Randomized Ball Carving is deterministic In each subproblem the leaders are sampled randomly (Line 3), thus once randomness is fixed the same leaders will always be sampled within a given subproblem. The next subproblems are given by computing an exact nearest neighbor computation on the leaders, and thus are completely determined by the leaders and subproblem points. Merging likewise can be done in such a way as to not depend on the scheduler. Lemma A.2. HashPrune is History Independent Let p ⊆ 𝒳 p\subseteq\mathcal{X} be an arbitrary point, and consider p p 's HashPrune reservoir R p = [ r 1 , … , r s ] R_{p}=[r_{1},\dots,r_{s}] with hash family H = { h 1 , … , h b } H={h_{1},\dots,h_{b}} . Suppose some fixed collection C = { c 1 , … , c i , … , c q } C={c_{1},\dots,c_{i},\dots,c_{q}} is to be inserted into R p R_{p} . We claim that R p R_{p} will contain the s s elements of C C nearest to p p such that they do not collide under H H . To see that this is true, consider that if we removed the size restriction of the reservoir entirely, then each position in R p R_{p} would just contain the nearest point in each hash bucket (lines 3-5 of HashPrune). Now, when we consider only the s s -sized reservoir, the further member of R p R_{p} is evicted in line 10 whenever the reservoir is full. Thus, the sequence of insertions is irrelevant to the final structure of R p R_{p} . A.9. Modifying MIRAGE In our experiments, we compare against the MIRAGE nearest neighbor index. For transparency, we point out that, at the time of writing this, the native MIRAGE codebase does not currently support datasets much larger than the 10 million scale, because indices are done using signed 32-bit integers. Thus, a maximum degree of 128, for example, will lead to an integer overflow on the number of edges when the number of vertices exceeds 33.6 million. In order to let MIRAGE run on our datasets, we modify the code to use unsigned 64-bit integers instead, which could impact the performance of the implementation. With that said, we see little noticeable effect on the build and query speeds of the graphs. Moreover, this size integer is used in the implementations of all other algorithms that we tested. Source: Postgres as Vector DB - a benchmark - Sean's Blog
URL: https://seanpedersen.github.io/posts/vector-databases/
Postgres as Vector DB - a benchmark - Sean's Blog Sean's Blog Postgres as Vector DB - a benchmark [-] Postgres as Vector DB - a benchmark PGVector HNSW IvfFlat IvfFlat Binary (with reranking) PGVectorScale DiskANN VectorChord VChordRQ VChordG (DiskANN) Benchmark ANN Benchmark Results Optimizing Vector & Index Storage Show me the code Conclusion References October 8, 2025 Edit on GitHub There is a flood of vector databases - which ones are actually useful? IMO extending a relational DBMS with ACID compliance and existing datasets, is for most use cases the ideal choice. Using a dedicated vector DB like ( Chroma, Vespa, Turbopuffer, LanceDB, Milvus etc.) only makes sense for narrow use cases where no complicated meta-data filters are needed (e.g. just simple RAG) and data synchronisation is no issue. So let's have a look how we can store and search vectors using Postgres - there are three notable extensions for Postgres: pgvector, pgvectorscale and vectorchord. PGVector Standard extension to store vectors with common medium scale ANN indices (HNSW & IvfFlat). Integration package for Python: https://github.com/pgvector/pgvector-python/ Store vectors (float 32 bit): Store half-precision (float 16 bit) vectors: HNSW Hierarchical navigable small world (HNSW) is a popular graph based ANN index - delivering good retrieval and QPS performance but at the cost of longer index build times and using more RAM for it (did crash for 1 million vectors on my 16GB RAM machine). IvfFlat Inverted File Flat (IvfFlat) is less RAM hungry than HNSW. With caveats: IvfFlat performance degrades as vectors are deleted and added (because centroids are not updated), thus needing regular index rebuilds (if new vectors get inserted regularly). "As data gets inserted or deleted from the index, if the index is not rebuilt, the IVFFlat index in pgvector can return incorrect approximate nearest neighbors due to clustering centroids no longer fitting the data well" IvfFlat Binary (with reranking) Build IvfFlat index on binarized vectors -> on query: overfetch Kx10 nearest neighbors, then rerank using float16/32 vectors to get more accurate final K-NN. Show More (34 lines) PGVectorScale DiskANN A promising ANN index that uses RAM and disk (needs fast disk - SSD / NVMe) to scale to billions of vectors, promising low RAM usage while still providing decent QPS. The problem is that the pgvectorscale index building implementation is single core right now - leading to very long index generation times. PGVectorScale supports pre-filtering using bitfields with manual meta-data table setup (complicated / bad dev ux). VectorChord VChordRQ A custom ANN index with excellent performance (combining IVF ANN index with RaBitQ quantization). Supports pre-filtering (easy to use). VChord can be configured to not copy all vectors into the index (which is the default and pgvector also does), reducing disk usage - it can be enabled (slightly degrading performance). VChord also supports efficient range filters (limiting results by distance instead of K nearest neighbors). VChordG (DiskANN) A novel addition (not prodution ready yet): custom implementation of DiskANN index combined with RaBitQ quantization. https://blog.vectorchord.ai/vectorchord-05-new-rabitq-empowered-diskann-index-and-continuous-recall-measurement?source=more_articles_bottom_blogs Benchmark The benchmark should reflect realistic usage - right now it just measures index build and query times. In the future I want to extend it: measure insertion perormance after initial index is built use real data embedding vectors -> simulate data distribution shift (insert many vectors after index was built) simulate realistic complex SQL queries involving categorical and range filtering benchmark vector scales: 100K, 1M, 10M, 100M, 1B ANN Benchmark Results The table below was computed using 100K CLIP Matryoshka text embeddings. Optimizing Vector & Index Storage When things scale up one should strive for efficient vector storage using: Vector Dimensionality Reduction Product Quantization (PQ): reduces both dimensionality and bit representation Matryoshka Embeddings: superior performance vs PQ as it learns the reduction in training and not post-training PCA / UMAP Scalar Quantization (Reduce bit representation per dimension) FP16: half precision from FP32 binary: reduce to single bit In pgvector: binary quantization will reduce any positive value to 1, and any zero or negative value to 0 Mean-based thresholding: binarize each dimension using its corpus-wide mean as the threshold, ensuring ~50/50 bit distribution per dimension for better information preservation and more discriminative binary codes. We can see that using IvfFlat index on binary representation with a top-K factor of 10x (overfetching), then reranking in higher precision (float32 or float16) results in excellent recall, low vector storage costs (float16) and very fast retrieval latencies. Though the reranking can lead to complications with queries using metadata filtering, so just using vchordrq index with 512D and float16 can also make sense. Show me the code Check out the code here Conclusion VectorChord is a good choice - providing superior performance and better developer experience (pre-filtering and better default settings). The vchordrq index is for most use cases the ideal choice as it delivers great performance and handles data distribution drift better than diskann indices. Using an ANN index only starts to make sense for big numbers of vectors (over 1 million). Prune, Don't Rebuild: Efficiently Tuning 𝛼-Reachable Graphs for Nearest Neighbor Search Back to arXiv

References License: CC BY 4.0 arXiv:2602.08097v1 [cs.DS] 08 Feb 2026 Prune, Don't Rebuild: Efficiently Tuning α \alpha -Reachable Graphs for Nearest Neighbor Search Report issue for preceding element Tian Zhang Ashwin Padaki Jiaming Liang Zack Ives Erik Waingarten University of Pennsylvania {tianzh, apadaki, liangjm, zives, ewaingar}@seas.upenn.edu Report issue for preceding element Abstract Report issue for preceding element Vector similarity search is an essential primitive in modern AI and ML applications. Most vector databases adopt graph-based approximate nearest neighbor (ANN) search algorithms, such as DiskANN [ 10] , which have demonstrated state-of-the-art empirical performance. DiskANN's graph construction is governed by a reachability parameter α \alpha , which gives a trade-off between construction time, query time, and accuracy. However, adaptively tuning this trade-off typically requires rebuilding the index for different α \alpha values, which is prohibitive at scale. In this work, we propose RP-Tuning, an efficient post-hoc routine, based on DiskANN's pruning step, to adjust the α \alpha parameter without reconstructing the full index. Within the α \alpha -reachability framework of prior theoretical works [ 8, 7] , we prove that pruning an initially α \alpha -reachable graph with RP-Tuning preserves worst-case reachability guarantees in general metrics and improved guarantees in Euclidean metrics. Empirically, we show that RP-Tuning accelerates DiskANN tuning on four public datasets by up to 43 × \times with negligible overhead. Report issue for preceding element 1 Introduction Report issue for preceding element Vector similarity search is an essential primitive in modern AI and ML applications, such as retrieval augmented generation [ 5] , linking and matching for knowledge graph construction [ 18] , and curating datasets for model training and fine-tuning. Most vector database implementations adopt graph-based approximate-nearest-neighbor (ANN) search algorithms. At scale, DiskANN [ 10] is widely used, e.g., in Milvus [ 17] and Microsoft's CosmosDB [ 16] , both because of its state-of-the-art empirical performance and its ability to scale the index structure beyond RAM (in contrast to more widely known algorithms like HNSW [ 12] ). A challenge with indices like DiskANN is that the tradeoffs between accuracy, latency, and space are not well understood — requiring users to empirically evaluate the indices under different hyperparameter settings, until the right tradeoffs are found. Report issue for preceding element To address the challenge, we propose RP-Tuning, an efficient post-hoc pruning algorithm derived from the RobustPrune subroutine used in the DiskANN index construction. RP-Tuning enables dynamic adjustment of DiskANN's reachability parameter, giving a tunable trade-off between accuracy, latency, and index size. By starting from a high-quality base graph, it avoids the cost of repeated index reconstruction. Leveraging recent theoretical frameworks for α \alpha -reachable graphs [ 8, 7] , we provide a rigorous analysis of RP-Tuning. Namely, we prove that a graph pruned via RP-Tuning preserves specific worst-case reachability guarantees in general metrics while achieving superior worst-case bounds in Euclidean metrics. Report issue for preceding element Our empirical evaluation across four public datasets demonstrates that RP-Tuning accelerates the DiskANN tuning process by up to 43 × \times . Moreover, indices tuned with RP-Tuning exhibit performance gains over those rebuilt with the same reachability parameters. Similarly to model distillation, our techniques allow for the “distillation” of an index into different configurations, suitable for different hardware capabilities, with predictable accuracy. Report issue for preceding element 2 Preliminaries Report issue for preceding element We consider a dataset P P in a metric space ( X , D ) (X,D) where X X is the universe of points and D D is the distance function. We use λ \lambda and Δ \Delta to denote the doubling dimension and the aspect ratio of P P . A graph-based ANN index for P P is given by a directed graph G = ( P , E ) G=(P,E) ; we use ( a , b ) (a,b) to denote the one-way edge from a a to b b . Note that we will abuse P P to refer to the dataset and the vertex set of the graph, since in this paper, points in the dataset have a one-to-one correspondence to vertices in the graph. We use N o  u  t  ( a ) , N i  n  ( a ) N_{out}(a),N_{in}(a) to denote the set of out-neighbors and in-neighbors, respectively, of point a ∈ P a\in P when the graph in the context is clear. Report issue for preceding element We give a brief review of the DiskANN algorithm and its theoretical guarantees below. We refer readers to [ 10, 8, 7] for full details. Report issue for preceding element 2.1 DiskANN Algorithm Review Report issue for preceding element There are multiple variants of DiskANN [ 6, 9, 1] . We review two variants that are relevant to this paper: the original Vamana heuristics proposed in [ 10] and the “slow preprocessing” variant formalized in [ 8] . Report issue for preceding element The search algorithm is the same for both variants (see Algorithm 2). Given the index graph, the search is initialized by seeding a candidate set ℒ \mathcal{L} with a designated start vertex s s (typically the medoid) and maintaining an empty set of visited vertices 𝒱 \mathcal{V} . In each iteration, the algorithm explores the graph by selecting the point p ∗ p^{} from the unexplored candidates in ℒ \mathcal{L} that is most proximal to the query q q . By immediately adding the out-neighbors N out  ( p ∗ ) N_{\text{out}}(p^{}) to the candidate set ℒ \mathcal{L} and marking p ∗ p^{} as visited, the algorithm effectively “walks” through the graph's edges toward the query. To ensure the search remains computationally tractable, the candidate set ℒ \mathcal{L} is restricted to a maximum size L L ; if the addition of neighbors causes the set to exceed this bound, ℒ \mathcal{L} is pruned to retain only the L L points closest to q q . This iterative process of greedy selection and neighborhood expansion continues until no unvisited points remain in the search beam, at which point the k k best-found candidates are returned.Report issue for preceding elementThe index construction of both variants relies on the subroutine RobustPrune (see Algorithm 1). RobustPrune prunes out-neighbor candidates for the input vertex p p and utilizes a parameter α ≥ 1 \alpha\geq 1 to favor “diverse” neighbors that provide long-range shortcuts across the dataset. The algorithm proceeds by iteratively selecting the point p ∗ p^{} in the candidate set 𝒱 \mathcal{V} that is closest to the target point p p . Once a candidate is added to N o  u  t  ( p ) N_{out}(p) , a pruning condition is applied to all remaining candidates: any point p ′ p^{\prime} that is closer to the newly added out-neighbor p ∗ p^{} than it is to the target point p p by a factor of α \alpha (i.e., D  ( p ∗ , p ′ ) < D  ( p , p ′ ) / α D(p^{},p^{\prime})<D(p,p^{\prime})/\alpha ) is removed from the candidate set 𝒱 \mathcal{V} . The intuition is that the edge ( p , p ′ ) (p,p^{\prime}) is potentially not important if moving from p to p decreases the distance to p' by a factor of α \alpha .Report issue for preceding elementThe “slow preprocessing” construction executes RobustPrune  ( p , P ∖ { p } , α , n − 1 ) \textsc{RobustPrune}(p,P\setminus{p},\alpha,n-1) for every vertex p p in the graph. Notice the candidate set 𝒱 = P ∖ { p } \mathcal{V}=P\setminus{p} , and the degree limit R = n − 1 R=n-1 , which results in an O  ( n 3 ) O(n^{3}) time complexity that is impractical at scale. However, it provides a rigorous structural foundation, α \alpha -reachability, for analyzing the algorithm's worst-case performance on construction time, query time, and approximation factor.Report issue for preceding elementDefinition 2.1 ( α \alpha -reachable and α \alpha -reachability).Report issue for preceding elementLet α > 1 \alpha>1 . We say, in a graph G = ( P , E ) G=(P,E) , q q is α \alpha -reachable from another vertex p p if either ( p , q ) ∈ E (p,q)\in E , or there exists p ′ p^{\prime} s.t. ( p , p ′ ) ∈ E (p,p^{\prime})\in E and D  ( p ′ , q ) ⋅ α ≤ D  ( p , q ) D(p^{\prime},q)\cdot\alpha\leq D(p,q) . We say a graph G G is α \alpha -reachable if any vertex in P P is α \alpha -reachable from any other vertex in P P .Report issue for preceding elementMoreover, we define the reachability of a graph G G as the maximum number α ∗ \alpha^{} such that G G is α ∗ \alpha^{} -reachable.Report issue for preceding elementThen, the following result on the “slow preprocessing” construction can be proved. Recall λ \lambda and Δ \Delta denote the dataset's doubling dimension and the aspect ratio.Report issue for preceding elementLemma 2.2 (Lemma 3.2 and 3.3 in [ 8] ).Report issue for preceding elementApplying RobustPrune  ( p , P ∖ { p } , α , ∞ ) \textsc{RobustPrune}(p,P\setminus{p},\alpha,\infty) for every p ∈ P p\in P yields an α \alpha -reachable graph with maximum degree O  ( ( 4  α ) λ  log  Δ ) O((4\alpha)^{\lambda}\log\Delta) .Report issue for preceding elementThey also provided a lower bound on worst-case search performance, which is later improved by [ 7] . In particular, we haveReport issue for preceding elementTheorem 2.3 (Theorem 1.1 in [ 7] ).Report issue for preceding elementLet G G be a α \alpha -reachable graph. For any query q q , any start point s s , any constant ϵ > 0 \epsilon>0 , and any k < L k<L , GreedySearch  ( s , q , k , L ) \textsc{GreedySearch}(s,q,k,L) in at most O  ( L + log α  Δ ( α − 1 )  ϵ ) O\left(L+\log_{\alpha}\frac{\Delta}{(\alpha-1)\epsilon}\right) steps outputs a set of points { b 1 , … , b L } {b_{1},\dots,b_{L}} such that each b j b_{j} satisfies:Report issue for preceding elementwhere a j a_{j} is the j j th nearest neighbor to q q .Report issue for preceding elementFrom the above, we can see the parameter α \alpha is a key parameter for the worst-case trade-off of the “slow preprocessing” DiskANN (an α \alpha -reachable graph), which hasReport issue for preceding element• Storage: O  ( n ⋅ ( 4  α ) λ  log  Δ ) O(n\cdot(4\alpha)^{\lambda}\log\Delta) Report issue for preceding element• Query Time: O  ( ( L + log α  Δ ( α − 1 )  ϵ ) ⋅ ( 4  α ) λ  log  Δ ) O\left(\left(L+\log_{\alpha}\frac{\Delta}{(\alpha-1)\epsilon}\right)\cdot(4\alpha)^{\lambda}\log\Delta\right) Report issue for preceding element• Approximation: ( ϵ + α α − 1 ) \left(\epsilon+\frac{\alpha}{\alpha-1}\right) for Euclidean metrics; ( ϵ + α + 1 α − 1 ) \left(\epsilon+\frac{\alpha+1}{\alpha-1}\right) for general metrics. Report issue for preceding elementAs α \alpha increases, storage and query time increase while the approximation factor decreases, indicating more accurate output.Report issue for preceding elementThe original Vamana index construction, on the other hand, caps the maximum out-degree of the graph for fast construction in practice. Besides RobustPrune, it uses other heuristics, which we will not discuss since we never open the black box in this paper and we refer readers to [ 10] for details. Despite no clear worst-case guarantee, Vamana can achieve state-of-the-art empirical performance, and the parameter α \alpha influences the empirical trade-off in the same directions as it does in the theoretical framework of the slow preprocessing variant.Report issue for preceding elementAlgorithm 1 RobustPrune ( p , 𝒱 , α , R p,\mathcal{V},\alpha,R )Input: Graph G G , point p ∈ P p\in P , \mathcal{V} ]Report issue for preceding element3 RP-Tuning and Worst-Case GuaranteesReport issue for preceding elementIn this section, we propose RP-Tuning, a simple yet disciplined pruning algorithm based on RobustPrune, which efficiently prunes an α \alpha -reachable graph G G into a sparser graph with a theoretical guarantee of worst-case performance trade-off.Report issue for preceding elementRP-TuningReport issue for preceding elementRP-Tuning takes an initially α 1 \alpha_{1} -reachable graph G = ( P , E ) G=(P,E) , and a new reachability target α 2 < α 1 \alpha_{2}<\alpha_{1} as inputs. It simply runs RobustPrune  ( p , N o  u  t  ( p ) , α 2 , ∞ ) \textsc{RobustPrune}(p,N_{out}(p),\alpha_{2},\infty) for every p ∈ P p\in P and outputs the modified graph G ′ = ( P , E ′ ) G^{\prime}=(P,E^{\prime}) .Report issue for preceding elementWe study the worst-case reachability of the output G ′ G^{\prime} of RP-Tuning  ( G , α 2 ) \textsc{RP-Tuning}(G,\alpha_{2}) where G G is an α 1 \alpha_{1} -reachable graph. It turns out that the worst-case reachability of G ′ G^{\prime} is a function of α 1 , α 2 \alpha_{1},\alpha_{2} and depends on the metric space and whether the pruning subroutine RobustPrune sorts the out-neighbors. To distinguish the unsorted version from RobustPrune, we use UnsortedRobustPrune to denote the procedure that is the same as RobustPrune but only replaces the step “ p ∗ ← arg  min p ′ ∈ 𝒱  D  ( p , p ′ ) p^{}\leftarrow\arg\min_{p^{\prime}\in\mathcal{V}}D(p,p^{\prime}) ” in Algorithm 1 with “ p ∗ p^{} being an arbitrary out-neighbor of p p ”. Our results on the reachability of G ′ G^{\prime} in different settings are summarized in Table 1, and formally stated in Theorem 3.1 and Theorem 3.2.Report issue for preceding elementTable 1: Worst-case reachability after RP-TuningReport issue for preceding elementNote 1 / ( 1 α 1 ⋅ 1 − 1 4  α 2 2 + 1 α 2 ⋅ 1 − 1 4  α 1 2 ) 1/({\frac{1}{\alpha_{1}}\cdot\sqrt{1-\frac{1}{4\alpha_{2}^{2}}}+\frac{1}{\alpha_{2}}\cdot\sqrt{1-\frac{1}{4\alpha_{1}^{2}}}}) is larger than 1 1 α 1 + 1 α 2 \frac{1}{\frac{1}{\alpha_{1}}+\frac{1}{\alpha_{2}}} . For a concrete example, when α 1 = 3 , α 2 = 2 \alpha_{1}=3,\alpha_{2}=2 , this expression ≈ 1.226 \approx 1.226 while α 1  α 2 α 1 + α 2 = 1.2 \frac{\alpha_{1}\alpha_{2}}{\alpha_{1}+\alpha_{2}}=1.2 .Report issue for preceding elementTheorem 3.1 (Unsorted Reachability).Report issue for preceding elementLet P P be a dataset in any metric space ( X , D ) (X,D) . Fix finite parameters α 1 ≥ α 2 > 1 \alpha_{1}\geq\alpha_{2}>1 . Let G = ( P , E ) G=(P,E) be any α 1 \alpha_{1} -reachable graph on P P . Let G ′ = ( P , E ′ ) G^{\prime}=(P,E^{\prime}) be the graph obtained by applying RobustPrune  ( p , N out  ( p ) , α 2 , ∞ ) \textsc{RobustPrune}(p,N_{\text{out}}(p),\alpha_{2},\infty) for all p ∈ P p\in P . Then, the graph G ′ G^{\prime} has reachability 1 / ( 1 / α 1 + 1 / α 2 + 1 ) 1/\left(1/\alpha_{1}+1/\alpha_{2}+1\right) in the worst case.Report issue for preceding elementTheorem 3.2 (Sorted Reachability).Report issue for preceding elementLet P P be a dataset in a metric space ( X , D ) (X,D) . Fix finite parameters α 1 ≥ α 2 > 1 \alpha_{1}\geq\alpha_{2}>1 . Let G = ( P , E ) G=(P,E) be any α 1 \alpha_{1} -reachable graph on P P . Let G ′ = ( P , E ′ ) G^{\prime}=(P,E^{\prime}) be the graph constructed by applying RobustPrune  ( p , N out  ( p ) , α 2 , ∞ ) \textsc{RobustPrune}(p,N_{\text{out}}(p),\alpha_{2},\infty) for all p ∈ P p\in P . Then the following holds in the worst case:Report issue for preceding element• For any metric space, G ′ G^{\prime} has reachability 1 / ( 1 / α 1 + 1 / α 2 ) 1/\left(1/\alpha_{1}+1/\alpha_{2}\right) . Report issue for preceding element• If ( X , D ) (X,D) is a Euclidean space, then G ′ G^{\prime} has reachability 1 / ( ( 1 / α 1 ) ⋅ 1 − 1 4  α 2 2 + ( 1 / α 2 ) ⋅ 1 − 1 4  α 1 2 ) 1/\left({(1/\alpha_{1})\cdot\sqrt{1-\frac{1}{4\alpha_{2}^{2}}}+(1/\alpha_{2})\cdot\sqrt{1-\frac{1}{4\alpha_{1}^{2}}}}\right) . Report issue for preceding elementReaders may find an analogy in our results to [ 7] , where Euclidean metrics and neighbor sorting guarantee a better worst-case reachability. In our problem, neighbor sorting alone already admits a better reachability, and it is further improved in Euclidean metrics. However, Euclidean metrics alone do not admit better reachability.Report issue for preceding elementThe reason why the reachability of the pruned graph G ′ G^{\prime} is crucial is as follows. First, the following lemma in [ 8] can help us to bound the maximum degree of the pruned graph G ′ G^{\prime} .Report issue for preceding elementLemma 3.3 (Lemma 3.3 in [ 8] ).Report issue for preceding elementFor any α > 1 \alpha>1 , graph G = ( P , E ) G=(P,E) , and p ∈ P p\in P , applying RobustPrune  ( p , N o  u  t  ( p ) , α , ∞ ) \textsc{RobustPrune}(p,N_{out}(p),\alpha,\infty) to every p ∈ P p\in P guarantees that the maximum degree of the modified graph is O  ( ( 4  α ) λ  log  Δ ) O((4\alpha)^{\lambda}\log\Delta) .Report issue for preceding elementUsing the above lemma, we know that the size of G ′ G^{\prime} is n ⋅ O  ( ( 4  α 2 ) λ  log  Δ ) n\cdot O((4\alpha_{2})^{\lambda}\log\Delta) . The query time of G ′ G^{\prime} now only depends on the number of steps during search, and the approximation of G ′ G^{\prime} can be quantified by its reachability via Theorem 2.3, where the larger the reachability is, the smaller both the number of steps and approximation are. Therefore, the rest of analysis on query time and approximation is reduced to exploring the reachability of G ′ G^{\prime} . We want it to be as large (or as close to α 2 \alpha_{2} ) as possible.Report issue for preceding elementNext, we give the proofs of the above two theorems and include the skipped proof in the appendix.Report issue for preceding element3.1 Proof of Theorem 3.1Report issue for preceding elementLet G = ( P , E ) G=(P,E) be an α 1 \alpha_{1} -reachable graph and let G ′ = ( P , E ′ ) G^{\prime}=(P,E^{\prime}) be obtained by applying UnsortedRobustPrune  ( p , ∅ , α 2 , ∞ ) \textsc{UnsortedRobustPrune}(p,\emptyset,\alpha_{2},\infty) at every p ∈ P p\in P .Report issue for preceding elementLet α ∗ \alpha^{} denote the reachability of G ′ G^{\prime} . Fix an ordered pair p , z p,z that is tight for α ∗ \alpha^{} -reachability and satisfies ( p , z ) ∉ E ′ (p,z)\notin E^{\prime} (such a pair must exist since α ∗ \alpha^{} is finite). Then, the directed edge ( p , z ) (p,z) was either (a) absent in G G , or (b) pruned from G G . In case (b), there is some edge ( p , y ) ∈ E (p,y)\in E for which D  ( y , z ) ≤ D  ( p , z ) / α 2 D(y,z)\leq D(p,z)/\alpha_{2} , which gives α ∗ ≥ α 2 \alpha^{\ast}\geq\alpha_{2} . Report issue for preceding element In case (a), α 1 \alpha_{1} -reachability of G G implies there is some ( p , y ) ∈ E (p,y)\in E such that D  ( y , z ) ≤ D  ( p , z ) / α 1 D(y,z)\leq D(p,z)/\alpha_{1} . This means that applying UnsortedRobustPrune with parameter α 2 \alpha_{2} either (a1) prunes ( p , y ) (p,y) from G ′ G^{\prime} , or (a2) there is some edge ( p , x ) ∈ E (p,x)\in E such that D  ( x , y ) ≤ D  ( p , y ) / α 2 D(x,y)\leq D(p,y)/\alpha_{2} . As before, in case (a1) it follows that α ∗ ≥ α 1 \alpha^{}\geq\alpha_{1} . In case (a2), the edge ( p , x ) (p,x) gives a lower bound on the reachability of G ′ G^{\prime} . Namely, α ∗ \alpha^{} is at least the value of the following optimization problem: Report issue for preceding element By invariance under scaling, we may take D  ( p , z ) = 1 D(p,z)=1 and flip the objective to get the following crucial lemma. Report issue for preceding element p p z z y y x x D  ( p , z ) = 1 D(p,z)=1 D  ( z , y ) = 1 α 1 D(z,y)=\frac{1}{\alpha_{1}} D  ( y , x ) = D  ( p , y ) α 2 D(y,x)=\frac{D(p,y)}{\alpha_{2}} Report issue for preceding element Figure 1: The setting that achieves the optimal objective in the optimization problem in Lemma 3.4. Report issue for preceding element The setting exactly makes the inequalities used in constructing the upper bound tight. In conclusion, we show the optimization problem solves to ( α 1 + α 2 + 1 ) / ( α 1  α 2 ) (\alpha_{1}+\alpha_{2}+1)/(\alpha_{1}\alpha_{2}) . By Lemma 3.4, the worst-case reachability is min  ( α 1 , α 2 , ( α 1  α 2 ) / ( α 1 + α 2 + 1 ) ) = ( α 1  α 2 ) / ( α 1 + α 2 + 1 ) \min(\alpha_{1},\alpha_{2},(\alpha_{1}\alpha_{2})/(\alpha_{1}+\alpha_{2}+1))=(\alpha_{1}\alpha_{2})/(\alpha_{1}+\alpha_{2}+1) . That completes the proof for Theorem 3.1. Report issue for preceding element Also notice that, in Figure 1, x x is the furthest point from p p , while z z is the closest one. Later we will see that this is a forbidden pattern when using RobustPrune instead of UnsortedRobustPrune, because x , y , z x,y,z have to be considered in order of their distances to p p in order to achieve an analogous case to ( a  2 ) (a2) . Report issue for preceding element 3.2 Proof Overview of Theorem 3.2 Report issue for preceding element Now we use RobustPrune where neighbors in the graph are considered for pruning in ascending order of their distances to the pivot point. The proof follows the same template as the unsorted case. The key difference is that, because RobustPrune processes candidates in increasing distance from p p , any point x x that can prune y y must be considered no later than y y , hence must satisfy D  ( p , x ) ≤ D  ( p , y ) D(p,x)\leq D(p,y) . Moreover, since y y serves as the α 1 \alpha_{1} -reachability witness for ( p , z ) (p,z) , we also have D  ( p , y ) ≤ D  ( p , z ) D(p,y)\leq D(p,z) . This yields the following lemma. Report issue for preceding element Lemma 3.6. Report issue for preceding element Let ( X , D  ( ⋅ , ⋅ ) ) (X,D(\cdot,\cdot)) be a metric space, and p , x , y , z ∈ X p,x,y,z\in X be four points. Given parameters satisfying α 1 ≥ α 2 ≥ 1 \alpha_{1}\geq\alpha_{2}\geq 1 , the worst-case reachability of the graph G ′ G^{\prime} obtained by taking an α 1 \alpha_{1} -reachable graph and applying RobustPrune  ( p , ∅ , α 2 , ∞ ) \textsc{RobustPrune}(p,\emptyset,\alpha_{2},\infty) at every p ∈ P p\in P is min  { α 2 , 1 / OPT } \min{\alpha_{2},1/\mathrm{OPT}} , where OPT \mathrm{OPT} is the value of the following optimization problem: Report issue for preceding element Notice that the only difference in the optimization problem is that there is an ordered condition in the last constraint, which rules out the pattern seen in the previous subsection. However, we can obtain an upper bound on the optimal objective of the problem in the same way using the triangle inequality and problem constraints: D  ( x , z ) ≤ D  ( x , y ) + D  ( y , z ) ≤ D  ( p , y ) / α 2 + 1 / α 1 ≤ 1 / α 2 + 1 / α 1 = ( α 1 + α 2 ) / ( α 1  α 2 ) D(x,z)\leq D(x,y)+D(y,z)\leq D(p,y)/\alpha_{2}+1/\alpha_{1}\leq 1/\alpha_{2}+1/\alpha_{1}=(\alpha_{1}+\alpha_{2})/(\alpha_{1}\alpha_{2}) . Note that the upper bound is smaller than the one in unsorted case. In the general metric space, the upper bound is actually tight. Report issue for preceding element Lemma 3.7. Report issue for preceding element In a general metric space ( X , D ) (X,D) , there exists a setting of four points p , x , y , z ∈ X p,x,y,z\in X that satisfies D  ( y , z ) ≤ 1 / α 1 , D  ( x , y ) ≤ D  ( p , y ) / α 2 , D  ( x , y ) ≤ D  ( p , y ) ≤ D  ( p , z ) = 1 D(y,z)\leq 1/\alpha_{1},D(x,y)\leq D(p,y)/\alpha_{2},D(x,y)\leq D(p,y)\leq D(p,z)=1 , and D  ( x , z ) = ( α 1 + α 2 ) / ( α 1  α 2 ) D(x,z)=(\alpha_{1}+\alpha_{2})/(\alpha_{1}\alpha_{2}) . Report issue for preceding element Proof. Report issue for preceding element The setting is defined by the following distance matrix: Report issue for preceding element p x y z p 0 x 1 0 y 1 1 α 1 0 z 1 α 1 + α 2 α 1  α 2 1 α 2 0 \begin{array}[]{r|cccc}&p&x&y&z\ \hline\cr p&0&&&\ x&1&0&&\ y&1&\frac{1}{\alpha_{1}}&0&\ z&1&\frac{\alpha_{1}+\alpha_{2}}{\alpha_{1}\alpha_{2}}&\frac{1}{\alpha_{2}}&0\ \end{array} It is easy to verify that the triangular inequalities and the problem constraints are satisfied. ∎ Report issue for preceding element Figure 2(a) shows the setting in the proof, where x , y , z x,y,z are on the (general metric) unit sphere centered at p p . Since the lower bound of D  ( x , z ) D(x,z) given by the above setting matches the upper bound, we can conclude that the worst-case reachability is ( α 1  α 2 ) / ( α 1 + α 2 ) (\alpha_{1}\alpha_{2})/(\alpha_{1}+\alpha_{2}) for general metric spaces by Lemma 3.6. Report issue for preceding element 1 1 1 1 1 1 1 / α 1 1/\alpha_{1} 1 / α 2 1/\alpha_{2} p p x x y y z z Report issue for preceding element (a) General Metrics Report issue for preceding element 1 1 1 1 1 1 1 / α 2 1/\alpha_{2} 1 / α 1 1/\alpha_{1} p p x x y y z z Report issue for preceding element (b) Euclidean Report issue for preceding element Figure 2: The settings that achieve optimal objectives in the optimization problem in Lemma 3.6 for (a) general metric spaces and (b) Euclidean metrics. Report issue for preceding element Unfortunately, the optimal setting for general metric spaces that requires x , y , z x,y,z to be simultaneously collinear and on a unit sphere is impossible in Euclidean metrics. This indicates that the upper bound derived only using the triangle inequality and the problem constraints is likely to be loose in Euclidean space. We resort to tools for semi-definite programming to solve for Euclidean space as in [ 7] and get the following lemma. Report issue for preceding element Lemma 3.8. Report issue for preceding element Let α 1 ≥ α 2 > 1 \alpha_{1}\geq\alpha_{2}>1 . The value of the optimization problem Report issue for preceding element is Report issue for preceding element 1 α 1  1 − 1 4  α 2 2 + 1 α 2  1 − 1 4  α 1 2 . \frac{1}{\alpha_{1}}\sqrt{1-\frac{1}{4\alpha_{2}^{2}}};+;\frac{1}{\alpha_{2}}\sqrt{1-\frac{1}{4\alpha_{1}^{2}}}. can still be obtained by p , x , y , z p,x,y,z on the same 2-dimensional plane in Euclidean metrics. In the optimal setting ( Figure 2(b)), intuitively speaking, the curvature of Euclidean unit sphere pushes x , z x,z towards each other, which results in a smaller maximum D  ( x , z ) D(x,z) , thus a larger worst-case reachability for sorted Euclidean metrics. Report issue for preceding element 4 Experimental Evaluation Report issue for preceding element We have shown theoretical guarantees of the α \alpha -reachable graphs pruned by RP-Tuning. In this section, we test RP-Tuning on DiskANN indices to evaluate its real-world performance. We will use 100 100 -recall@ 100 100 , a widely adopted metric in practice, to measure query accuracy. 100 100 -Recall@ 100 100 is the proportion of the true top 100 100 nearest neighbors contained in the top 100 100 results returned by the algorithm. Report issue for preceding element 4.1 Setup Report issue for preceding element Hardware. We conduct all experiments on a cloud instance equipped with an AMD EPYC 9B14 CPU (15 cores, 30 threads) and 256GB RAM, running Debian GNU/Linux 12. Report issue for preceding element Datasets. We evaluate our methods on four benchmark datasets obtained from the BigANN benchmark repository [ 14] . SIFT-1M and GIST-1M [ 11] are classical computer vision datasets; the former consists of 1 million 128-dimensional SIFT feature vectors with 10,000 query vectors, while the latter contains 1 million 960-dimensional GIST descriptors capturing global image structure, with 1,000 query vectors. Deep-1M [ 2] comprises 1 million 96-dimensional vectors derived from deep neural network embeddings, with 10,000 query vectors. Finally, MSSPACEV-1M is a subset of 1 million 100-dimensional vectors from Microsoft's Bing query embeddings [ 14] , originally stored as int8 and converted to float32, with 29,316 query vectors. All datasets use Euclidean distance as the similarity metric. Report issue for preceding element Tuning Scheme Configuration Report issue for preceding element We implemented the original DiskANN (Vamana) 1 1 1 The index is called Vamana in [ 10] and “fast preprocessing version” in [ 8] . in [ 10] in Python. We construct the DiskANN indices for the above four datasets with the same parameter setting in [ 10] : α 1 = 1.2 , R = 70 , L − B  u  i  l  d = 75 \alpha_{1}=1.2,R=70,L-Build=75 . They are referred to as the base or meta indices in the rest of this section. Then we tune the base indices via RP-Tuning and rebuild with α 2 = 1.1 , 1.05 , 1.01 \alpha_{2}=1.1,1.05,1.01 . During query time, we use Algorithm 2 with the start vertex s s being the medoid of the dataset, k = 100 k=100 (since we will use 100 100 -Recall@ 100 100 to measure accuracy). We vary the index search effort by tuning the beam size parameter L L of Algorithm 2 as a knob to obtain different trade-offs between recall and query latency. Report issue for preceding element 4.2 Comparison between RP-Tuning and Rebuild for Tuning Report issue for preceding element Table 2: Runtime comparison: Rebuild vs. RP-Tuning (seconds). The rightmost columns show the cumulative time and overall speedup (Total T rebuild / Total  T prune T_{\text{rebuild}}/\text{Total }T_{\text{prune}} ) across all three configurations. Report issue for preceding element Report issue for preceding element Figure 3: Recall-QPS trade-off frontiers achieved by a base DiskANN graph of α 1 = 1.2 \alpha_{1}=1.2 then pruned graphs from the base graph via RP-Tuning with α 2 = 1.1 , 1.05 , 1.01 \alpha_{2}=1.1,1.05,1.01 , and the same base DiskANN graph of α = 1.2 \alpha=1.2 along with rebuilt DiskANN graphs of α = 1.1 , 1.05 , 1.01 \alpha=1.1,1.05,1.01 . Report issue for preceding element We compare the performance of RP-Tuning and rebuild for tuning the base index with three new alpha values by total processing time cost and recall-QPS trade-off. Report issue for preceding element Table 2 demonstrates the tuning efficiency of RP-Tuning. Our method achieves significant speedups compared to the rebuilding baseline across all datasets. Notably, the speed-up is the most significant on GIST1M (achieving up to 43 × 43\times total speedup). We attribute this performance to GIST1M's inherently sparser graph structure: at α = 1.2 \alpha=1.2 , the index averages only 29 edges per node, compared to 52 for MsSpace1M. While this lower edge density significantly reduces the pruning workload for RP-Tuning, the rebuilding baseline fails to exploit this structural sparsity because it remains latent to the algorithm until the graph construction is complete, resulting in substantially higher computational overhead. Report issue for preceding element Figure 3 illustrates the Pareto frontiers for the Recall-QPS trade-off, derived by combining the base index with either pruned indices or rebuilt indices. The composite frontier traces the base index curve to its maximum achievable QPS before transitioning to the curve segment of the next pruning or rebuilding configuration. Across all four datasets, the frontier generated by RP-Tuning (Prune Frontier) consistently outperforms the Rebuild Frontier. Notably, on the SIFT1M dataset, the rebuilt indices are entirely dominated by the base graph and fail to extend the efficiency frontier. These prune frontiers can be reconstructed by connecting the upper-right segments of the individual curves presented in Figure 4. Report issue for preceding element 4.3 Performance of Individual Indices Returned by RP-Tuning Report issue for preceding element Report issue for preceding element Figure 4: Recall-QPS trade-off frontiers achieved by a base DiskANN graph of α 1 = 1.2 \alpha_{1}=1.2 (blue curves with circles) and pruned graphs (curves with squares) from the base graph of α 2 = 1.1 , 1.05 , 1.01 \alpha_{2}=1.1,1.05,1.01 . Average degrees (Deg) of individual graphs are also included. Report issue for preceding element Report issue for preceding element Figure 5: Compare Recall-QPS performance of pruned (solid curves) and rebuilt (dashed curves) DiskANN indices. Pruned and rebuilt indices of the same α \alpha value share a color. Average degrees (Deg) of individual graphs are also included. Report issue for preceding element We also investigate the performance of individual indices produced by RP-Tuning and rebuild during the tuning experiments, in order to obtain a better understanding of the effects of RP-Tuning. Report issue for preceding element Figure 4 illustrates the performance of indices returned by RP-Tuning compared to the base indices. Starting with a base DiskANN index constructed with α = 1.2 \alpha=1.2 , we apply progressive pruning using α ∈ { 1.1 , 1.05 , 1.01 } \alpha\in{1.1,1.05,1.01} . The results demonstrate a clear trade-off between retrieval accuracy and query throughput: reducing α \alpha increases graph sparsity, which yields higher Queries Per Second (QPS) at the cost of marginal drops in Recall@100. While the denser graph excels in high-recall regimes, the sparser variants significantly reduce memory overhead and extend the maximum QPS, making them highly effective for resource-constrained local deployments or latency-critical applications. The consistent deviation of the pruned index curves from the baseline frontier suggests that RP-Tuning preserves the structural integrity of the base graph during pruning. Report issue for preceding element Figure 5 compares the performance of indices pruned via RP-Tuning against those rebuilt from scratch using identical α \alpha settings. While Theorem 3.2 indicates that pruned and rebuilt indices possess different theoretical worst-case guarantees, this comparison serves to evaluate how effectively pruning approximates the structure of a graph constructed directly with the target sparsity. Report issue for preceding element Perhaps surprisingly, even if an initially α 1 \alpha_{1} -reachable pruned by RP-Tuning with α 2 \alpha_{2} has less-than- α 2 \alpha_{2} reachability or even vacuous reachability (i.e., the reachability could be less than 1 1 in the worst case) in the worst case as shown in the last section, for Vamana heuristics and real-world datasets, we see that, across all four datasets, indices pruned via RP-Tuning consistently exhibit a superior QPS-recall trade-off compared to their rebuilt counterparts at the same α \alpha . This performance gap stems from the initialization quality: RP-Tuning operates on a base graph ( α = 1.2 \alpha=1.2 ) characterized by high connectivity and reachability, preserving more critical edges. Conversely, rebuilding from scratch with a small α \alpha constrains the graph's connectivity and reachability throughout the construction process, hindering the identification of optimal edges necessary for efficient navigation. Report issue for preceding element The structural deficiency of the rebuilt indices is particularly evident in SIFT1M for α = 1.01 , 1.05 , 1.10 \alpha=1.01,1.05,1.10 . In these cases, we observe a counter-intuitive phenomenon where increasing the search effort (beam size) reduces both QPS and recall simultaneously. This indicates that due to poor graph quality, a larger search beam size causes the query traversal to be misguided into local optima or irrelevant subgraphs rather than converging on the true nearest neighbors. Report issue for preceding element 5 Related Work Report issue for preceding element Approximate nearest-neighbor index structures are core to every vector database system, and their importance has motivated recent NeurIPS challenges [ 14, 13] . Early real-world systems leveraged the FAISS library [ 4] , often using its implementation of the Hierarchical Navigable Small Worlds [ 12] (HNSW) algorithm. However, few theoretical properties are known about HNSW, and it targets scenarios where the entire index fits into RAM. Other techniques more appropriate to disk-based storage, such as ANNOY [ 15] (based on random hyperplane splits into memory-mapped files), and inverted-list-based methods, such as SPANN [ 3] , are not well understood theoretically. DiskANN [ 6, 10, 1] remains a commonly used, state-of-the-art method for disk-based indexing. Its graph-based approach has been studied theoretically [ 8, 7] . Our work builds upon the formal understanding of DiskANN, but focuses on leveraging the theoretical insights to obtain practical index tuning.
